      <p>Sierra Leone is home to about sixteen <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups">ethnic groups</a>, each with its own language. The largest and most influential are the <a href="/wiki/Temne_people" title="Temne people">Temne</a> at about 32.2% and the <a href="/wiki/Mende_people" title="Mende people">Mende</a> at about 31.6%. The Temne predominate in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Province,_Sierra_Leone" title="Northern Province, Sierra Leone">Northern Sierra Leone</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Area,_Sierra_Leone" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Area, Sierra Leone">some areas around the capital of Sierra Leone</a>. The Mende predominate in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Province,_Sierra_Leone" title="Southern Province, Sierra Leone">South</a>-Eastern Sierra Leone (with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Kono_District" title="Kono District">Kono District</a>). </p>
      <p>The vast majority of Temne are Muslims at over 85%; and with a significant Christian minority at about 10%. The Mende are also Muslim majority at about 70%, though with a large Christian minority at about 30%. Sierra Leone's national politics centres on the competition between the north-west, dominated by the Temne, and the south-east dominated by the Mende. The vast majority of the Mende support the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_People%27s_Party" title="Sierra Leone People's Party">Sierra Leone People's Party</a> (SLPP); while the majority of the Temne support the <a href="/wiki/All_People%27s_Congress" title="All People's Congress">All People's Congress</a> (APC).<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Mende, who are believed to be descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Mane_people" title="Mane people">Mane</a>, originally occupied the Liberian hinterland. They began moving into Sierra Leone slowly and peacefully in the eighteenth century. The Temne are said to migrated from <a href="/wiki/Futa_Jallon" class="mw-redirect" title="Futa Jallon">Futa Jallon</a>, which is in present-day <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a>. </p>
      <p>The third-largest ethnic group are the <a href="/wiki/Limba_people_(Sierra_Leone)" title="Limba people (Sierra Leone)">Limba</a> at about 8.4% of the population. The Limba are <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">native</a> people of Sierra Leone. They have no tradition of origin, and it is believed that they have lived in Sierra Leone since before the European encounter. The Limba are primarily found in Northern Sierra Leone, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Bombali_District" title="Bombali District">Bombali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kambia_District" title="Kambia District">Kambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koinadugu_District" title="Koinadugu District">Koinadugu District</a>. The Limba are about 60% Christian and 40% Muslims. The Limba are close political allies of the neighbouring Temne. </p>
      <p>Since independence, the Limba have traditionally been very influential in Sierra Leone's politics, along with the Mende. The vast majority of Limba support the All People's Congress (APC) political party. Sierra Leone's first and second presidents, <a href="/wiki/Siaka_Stevens" title="Siaka Stevens">Siaka Stevens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Saidu_Momoh" title="Joseph Saidu Momoh">Joseph Saidu Momoh</a>, respectively, were both ethnic Limba. Sierra Leone's current <a href="/wiki/Defense_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense Minister">Defense Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Conteh" class="mw-redirect" title="Paolo Conteh">Alfred Paolo Conteh</a> is an ethnic Limba. </p>
      <p>One of the biggest minority ethnic groups are the <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula</a> at around 3.8% of the population. Descendants of seventeenth- and eighteenth-century Fula migrant settlers from the <a href="/wiki/Fouta_Djalon" class="mw-redirect" title="Fouta Djalon">Fouta Djalon</a> region of Guinea, they live primarily in the northeast and the western area of Sierra Leone. The Fula are virtually all Muslims at over 99%. The Fula are primarily <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">traders</a>, and many live in middle-class homes. Because of their trading, the Fulas are found in nearly all parts of the country. </p>
      <p>The other ethnic groups are the <a href="/wiki/Mandingo_people_of_Sierra_Leone" title="Mandingo people of Sierra Leone">Mandingo</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Mandinka</a>). They are descendants of <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">traders</a> from Guinea who migrated to Sierra Leone during the late nineteenth to mid-twentieth centuries. The Mandika are predominantly found in the east and the northern part of the country. They predominate in the large towns, most notably <a href="/wiki/Karina,_Sierra_Leone" title="Karina, Sierra Leone">Karina</a>, in Bombali District in the north; <a href="/wiki/Kabala,_Sierra_Leone" title="Kabala, Sierra Leone">Kabala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Falaba" title="Falaba">Falaba</a> in Koinadugu District in the north; and <a href="/wiki/Yengema" title="Yengema">Yengema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kono_District" title="Kono District">Kono District</a> in the east of the country. Like the Fula, the Mandinka are virtually all Muslims at over 99℅. Sierra Leone's third president <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Tejan_Kabbah" title="Ahmad Tejan Kabbah">Ahmad Tejan Kabbah</a>, and Sierra Leone's first Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Sorie_Ibrahim_Koroma" title="Sorie Ibrahim Koroma">Sorie Ibrahim Koroma</a> were both ethnic Mandingo. </p>
      <p>Next in proportion are the <a href="/wiki/Kono_people" title="Kono people">Kono</a>, who live primarily in <a href="/wiki/Kono_District" title="Kono District">Kono District</a> in Eastern Sierra Leone. The Kono are descendants of migrants from Guinea; today their workers are known primarily as diamond miners. The majority of the Kono ethnic group are Christians, though with an influential Muslim minority. Sierra Leone's former Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sam-Sumana" title="Samuel Sam-Sumana">Alhaji Samuel Sam-Sumana</a> is an ethnic Kono. </p>
      <p>The small but significant <a href="/wiki/Krio_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Krio people">Krio people</a> (descendants of freed African American, <a href="/wiki/West_Indian" title="West Indian">West Indian</a> and Liberated African slaves who settled in Freetown between 1787 and about 1885) make up about 3% of the population. They primarily occupy the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a> and its surrounding <a href="/wiki/Western_Area" title="Western Area">Western Area</a>. Krio culture reflects the Western culture and ideals within which many of their ancestors originated – they also had close ties with British officials and colonial administration during years of development. </p>
      <p>The Krio have traditionally dominated Sierra Leone's judiciary and Freetown's elected city council. One of the first ethnic groups to become educated according to Western traditions, they have traditionally been appointed to positions in the civil service, beginning during the colonial years. They continue to be influential in the civil service. The vast majority of Krios are Christians at about 90%, though with a small but significant Muslim minority at 10% known locally as Oku. </p>
      <p>Other minority ethnic groups are the <a href="/wiki/Kuranko_people" title="Kuranko people">Kuranko</a>, who are related to the Mandingo, and are largely Muslims. The Kuranko are believed to have begun arriving in Sierra Leone from Guinea in about 1600 and settled in the north, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Koinadugu_District" title="Koinadugu District">Koinadugu District</a>. The Kuranko are primarily farmers; leaders among them have traditionally held several senior positions in the Military. The current Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Sierra_Leone" title="Bank of Sierra Leone">Bank of Sierra Leone</a> <a href="/wiki/Kaifala_Marah" title="Kaifala Marah">Kaifala Marah</a> is an ethnic Kuranko. The Kuranko are largely Muslim majority. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Loko_people" title="Loko people">Loko</a> in the north are native people of Sierra Leone, believed to have lived in Sierra Leone since the time of European encounter. Like the neighbouring Temne, the Loko are Muslim majority. The <a href="/wiki/Susu_people" title="Susu people">Susu</a> and their related <a href="/wiki/Yalunka_people" title="Yalunka people">Yalunka</a> are traders; both groups are primarily found in the far north in Kambia and Koinadugu District close to the border with Guinea. The Susu and Yalunka kingdom was established in the early 5th 7th century before Mali empire, which was extended from Mali, Senegal, Guinea Bissau, Guinea Conakry to the northern part of Sierra Leone. They are the original owners of the Futa Djallon region covered by a vars land area both the Susu and Yalunka people are descendants of the Mande people. They are virtually all Muslims. The Yalunka, also spelled Jallonke, Yalonga, Djallonké, Djallonka or Dialonké, are a Mande people who have lived in the Djallon, a mountainous region in Sierra Leone, Mali, Senegal, Guinea Bissau and Guinea Conakry West Africa over 520 years ago. The name Yalunka literally means "inhabitants of the Jallon (mountains)". Manga Sewa was born in Falaba, Solima chiefdom, in the Northern Province of British Sierra Leone to Yalunka parents. His father was a Yalunka paramount chief of Solima, a prosperous chieftaincy. Its capital, Falaba, was on the rich trading routes leading to the coast. Manga Sewa's father had a number of wives and dozens of children.are traders; both groups are primarily found in the far north in <a href="/wiki/Kambia_District" title="Kambia District">Kambia</a> and Koinadugu District close to the border with Guinea. The Susu and Yalunka are both descendants of migrants from Guinea; and they both are virtually all Muslims at over 99%. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Kissi_people" title="Kissi people">Kissi</a> live further inland in South-Eastern Sierra Leone. They predominate in the large town of <a href="/wiki/Koindu" title="Koindu">Koindu</a> and its surrounding areas in Kailahun District. The vast majority of Kissi are Christians. The much smaller <a href="/wiki/Vai_people" title="Vai people">Vai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kru_people" title="Kru people">Kru</a> peoples are primarily found in <a href="/wiki/Kailahun_District" title="Kailahun District">Kailahun</a> and Pujehun Districts near the border with Liberia. The Kru predominate in the Kroubay neighbourhood in the capital Freetown. The Vai are largely Muslim majority at about 90%, while the Kru are virtually all Christians at over 99%. </p>
      <p>On the coast in <a href="/wiki/Bonthe_District" title="Bonthe District">Bonthe District</a> in the south are the <a href="/wiki/Sherbro_people" title="Sherbro people">Sherbro</a>. Native to Sierra Leone, they have occupied <a href="/wiki/Sherbro_Island" title="Sherbro Island">Sherbro Island</a> since it was founded. The Sherbro are primarily <a href="/wiki/Fisherman" title="Fisherman">fisherman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Farmers" class="mw-redirect" title="Farmers">farmers</a>, and they are predominantly found in Bonthe District. The Sherbro are virtually all Christians, and their paramount chiefs had a history of intermarriage with British colonists and traders. </p>
      <p>A small number of Sierra Leoneans are of partial or full <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people" title="Lebanese people">Lebanese</a> ancestry, descendants of traders who first came to the nation in the 19th century. They are locally known as Sierra Leonean-Lebanese. The Sierra Leonean-Lebanese community are primarily traders and they mostly live in middle-class households in the urban areas, primarily in <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bo,_Sierra_Leone" title="Bo, Sierra Leone">Bo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenema" title="Kenema">Kenema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koidu" title="Koidu">Koidu Town</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makeni" title="Makeni">Makeni</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_equality">Gender equality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Gender equality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Household">Household</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Household">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Although women account for about 50 per cent of the population in Sierra Leone, only 28 per cent are household heads.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-175">[175]</a></sup> As in the rest of the countries, education is a key factor in succeeding in aspects such as a well-paid job and covering the needs of a house. Rural areas are the most common to lack access to education having only male-headed four per cent ahead of females with basic education and 1.2 per cent more at post-graduate level.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-175">[175]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Sierra Leone, normally, men are automatically positioned as household heads; and their status does not change if their marital status changes over time.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-175">[175]</a></sup> However, female household does change depending on their marital status. A woman can be the head of the house only if she remains single for the rest of her life. But if a woman gets married, she will not be entitled as the head of the house any more.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_175-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-175">[175]</a></sup> Females can take over the household head if they become widowed or divorced.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_175-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-175">[175]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the labour field, it is expected that the household will financially provide the needs of the family. However, females face gender discrimination making them the target of lower incomes and financial struggle.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_175-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-175">[175]</a></sup> In numbers, females present a lower per cent (6.3) versus males (15.2) when it comes to be paid employees.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_175-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-175">[175]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War">War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Children who have been forced to be part of a war have experienced severe mental and emotional damage in Sierra Leone. However, the damage and way to deal with the effects of war depends on the gender of the kids. Both genders experienced and were involved in high levels of violence. Females, experiencing higher levels of rapes, presented greater signs of depression and anxiety.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-176">[176]</a></sup> Males, on the other hand, presented higher levels of anxiety and hostility.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-176">[176]</a></sup> Males also showed to be more vulnerable to depression after losing a care giver.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-176">[176]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Epidemics_.28Ebola.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemics_(Ebola)">Epidemics (Ebola)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Epidemics (Ebola)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>With women being more than half the population of Sierra Leone, they contribute in great part to the country's economy and society.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-177">[177]</a></sup> Sierra Leonean women in care-giving positions were particularly susceptible to the disease because of the potential close contact with infected individuals. If and when the infected person died, women were in charge of preparing the funeral, which involves touching the infected body which in turn gave women a high risk of becoming infected.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-177">[177]</a></sup> The vulnerability in infected women with Ebola grows when health institutions cannot provide a high quality service because they are full or do not have the right equipment to treat the patients.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-177">[177]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender-Based_Violence">Gender-Based Violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Gender-Based Violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In certain societies such as Sierra Leone, the lack of social and economic support for women makes them more vulnerable, making them the target of <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">gender violence</a>. By managing to repress women, men possess greater power and become the decision-maker.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-178">[178]</a></sup> &nbsp;The method to implement gender-based violence can vary; it can be sexual, physical, verbal, economical, or emotional.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-178">[178]</a></sup> Violence does not respect factors such as age, socio-economical position, or education.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_178-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-178">[178]</a></sup> Denouncing violence is hard for most women due to the honour and reputation of their family name.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_178-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-178">[178]</a></sup> A victim, who suffers from violence, begins to lose self-confidence due to fear of reporting the aggressor or exposing family problems.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_178-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-178">[178]</a></sup> This is why a woman who suffers from physical violence prefers not to attend with a specialist to heal her wounds.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_178-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-178">[178]</a></sup> In some other cases, when females dare to denounce the crime, the system is very corrupt that authorities involved accept bribes from the victimiser.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_178-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-178">[178]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Female_Economy">Female Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Female Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Small female-operated business not only could contribute to Sierra Leone's economy, but could also help women to become independent in several ways. However, women face discrimination when it comes to obtaining financial, social, and cultural help to start a business.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-179">[179]</a></sup> It is difficult to avoid the economic paralysis in Sierra Leone given that more than half of the population in the country is women.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-179">[179]</a></sup> Due to the lack of access to basic education, women are the least prepared when it comes to processing business licences, registering names or contracting.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-179">[179]</a></sup> Not having the capital to start a new business is the biggest barrier for women.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_179-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-179">[179]</a></sup> With the lack of technology, mostly in all Sierra Leone, it is hard to help a business to function.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_179-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-179">[179]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Sierra_Leone" title="Education in Sierra Leone">Education in Sierra Leone</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg/220px-Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg/330px-Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg/440px-Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="294"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Classroom_at_a_seconday_school_in_Pendembu_Sierra_Leone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A secondary school class in <a href="/wiki/Pendembu" class="mw-redirect" title="Pendembu">Pendembu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kailahun_District" title="Kailahun District">Kailahun District</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Education in Sierra Leone is legally required for all children for six years at <a href="/wiki/Primary_education" title="Primary education">primary level</a> (Class P1-P6) and three years in junior secondary education,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> but a shortage of schools and teachers has made implementation impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-56">[56]</a></sup> Two thirds of the adult population of the country are illiterate.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Civil_War" title="Sierra Leone Civil War">Sierra Leone Civil War</a> resulted in the destruction of 1,270 primary schools, and in 2001, 67% of all school-age children were out of school.<sup id="cite_ref-ilab_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilab-56">[56]</a></sup> The situation has improved considerably since then with primary school enrolment doubling between 2001 and 2005 and the reconstruction of many schools since the end of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Students at primary schools are usually 6 to 12 years old, and in secondary schools 13 to 18. Primary education is free and <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_education" title="Compulsory education">compulsory</a> in government-sponsored <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(government_funded)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public school (government funded)">public schools</a>. </p>
      <p>The country has three universities: <a href="/wiki/Fourah_Bay_College" title="Fourah Bay College">Fourah Bay College</a>, founded in 1827 (the oldest university in West Africa),<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> University of Makeni (established initially in September 2005 as The Fatima Institute, the college was granted university status in August 2009, and assumed the name University of Makeni, or UNIMAK), and <a href="/wiki/Njala_University" title="Njala University">Njala University</a>, primarily located in <a href="/wiki/Bo_District" title="Bo District">Bo District</a>. Njala University was established as the Njala Agricultural Experimental Station in 1910 and became a university in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Teacher training colleges and religious seminaries are found in many parts of the country. <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> grants scholarships to Sierra Leone students as part of its international development cooperation program.<sup id="cite_ref-jpost.com_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jpost.com-185">[185]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Sierra_Leone" title="Health in Sierra Leone">Health in Sierra Leone</a>
      </div>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> estimated that the average life expectancy in Sierra Leone was 57.39 years.<sup id="cite_ref-iuonoa_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iuonoa-186">[186]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The prevalence of <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> in the population is 1.6%, higher than the world average of 1% but lower than the average of 6.1% across <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Medical care is not readily accessible, with doctors and hospitals out of reach for many villagers. While free health care may be provided in some villages, the medical staff is poorly paid and sometimes charge for their services, taking advantage of the fact that the villagers are not aware of their right to free medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A dialysis machine, the first of its kind in the country, was donated by <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jpost.com_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jpost.com-185">[185]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to an <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a> report, private health expenditure accounts for 85.7% of total spending on health.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_medical_response">Emergency medical response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Emergency medical response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Having had no formal <a href="/wiki/Emergency_medical_services" title="Emergency medical services">emergency medical services</a> previously, the <a href="/wiki/First_Responder_Coalition_of_Sierra_Leone" title="First Responder Coalition of Sierra Leone">First Responder Coalition of Sierra Leone</a> (FRCSL) was formed in June 2019 in <a href="/wiki/Makeni" title="Makeni">Makeni</a> to facilitate the development of <a href="/wiki/Emergency_medical_responder" title="Emergency medical responder">emergency first responder</a> programs nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-190">[190]</a></sup> The founding members of the Coalition included the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Red_Cross_Society" title="Sierra Leone Red Cross Society">Sierra Leone Red Cross Society</a> (the first chairing organisation), <a href="/wiki/LFR_International" title="LFR International">LFR International</a> (proposed the formation), the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Makeni" title="University of Makeni">University of Makeni</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Agency_for_Rural_Community_Transformation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Agency for Rural Community Transformation (page does not exist)">Agency for Rural Community Transformation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_Hospital_(Makeni)" title="Holy Spirit Hospital (Makeni)">Holy Spirit Hospital</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-190">[190]</a></sup> The establishment of the Coalition was concurrent with the declaration by the 72nd World Health Assembly that emergency care systems are essential to universal health coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Between June and July 2019, the FRCSL trained 1,000 community members from Makeni to be first responders and equipped each trainee with a first aid kit.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endemic_and_infectious_diseases">Endemic and infectious diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Endemic and infectious diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Sierra Leone suffers from <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a> outbreaks of diseases, including <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lassa_fever" title="Lassa fever">lassa fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PHChand_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHChand-194">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">Yellow fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> are endemic to Sierra Leone.<sup id="cite_ref-PHChand_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHChand-194">[194]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternal_and_child_health">Maternal and child health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Maternal and child health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>According to 2010 estimates, Sierra Leone has the 1st highest <a href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternal mortality">maternal mortality</a> rate in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> According to a 2013 UNICEF report,<sup id="cite_ref-UNICEF2013p27_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICEF2013p27-196">[196]</a></sup> 88% of women in Sierra Leone have undergone <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Sierra Leone was estimated as having the 11th highest <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> rate in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>One of the consequences women in Sierra Leone face after a prolonged and obstructed labour which would have required a c-section is <a href="/wiki/Obstetric_fistula" title="Obstetric fistula">obstetric fistula</a>. This condition often drives women into poverty and isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The AWC- Aberdeen Women's Centre in <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a>, the second busiest hospital in Sierra Leone, delivering up to 3000 babies each year.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> The centre provides free surgery for women suffering from this condition. </p>
      <p>The centre provides a variety of maternal and child health services and is supported by not for profit organisations such as Freedom from Fistula,<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> The Aminata Maternal Foundation,<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> and UNFPA.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mental_health">Mental health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Mental health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">Mental healthcare</a> in Sierra Leone is almost non-existent. Many sufferers try to cure themselves with the help of traditional healers.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Civil_War" title="Sierra Leone Civil War">Civil War (1991–2002)</a>, many soldiers took part in atrocities and many children were forced to fight. This left them traumatised, with an estimated 400,000 people (by 2009) being mentally ill. Thousands of former child soldiers have fallen into substance abuse as they try to blunt their memories.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potable_water_supply">Potable water supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Potable water supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_in_Sierra_Leone" title="Water supply in Sierra Leone">Water supply in Sierra Leone</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Water supply in Sierra Leone is characterised by limited access to safe drinking water. Despite efforts by the government and numerous non-governmental organisations, access has not much improved since the end of the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Civil_War" title="Sierra Leone Civil War">Sierra Leone Civil War</a> in 2002, stagnating at about 50% and even declining in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-JMP_water_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JMP_water-205">[205]</a></sup> It is hoped that a new dam in Orugu, for which China committed financing in 2009, will alleviate <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water scarcity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Orugu_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orugu-206">[206]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to a national survey carried out in 2006, 84% of the urban population and 32% of the rural population had access to an <a href="/wiki/Improved_water_source" title="Improved water source">improved water source</a>. Those with access in rural areas were served almost exclusively by protected wells. The 68% of the rural population without access to an improved water source relied on surface water (50%), unprotected wells (9%) and unprotected springs (9%). Only 20% of the urban population and 1% of the rural population had access to piped drinking water in their home. Compared to the 2000 survey access has increased in urban areas, but has declined in rural areas, possibly because facilities have broken down because of a lack of maintenance.<sup id="cite_ref-JMP_water_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JMP_water-205">[205]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pushak_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pushak-207">[207]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>With a new decentralisation policy, embodied in the Local Government Act of 2004, responsibility for water supply in areas outside the capital was passed from the central government to local councils. In Freetown the Guma Valley Water Company remains in charge of water supply. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg/350px-Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="350" height="411" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg/525px-Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg/700px-Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1128"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Guinea_Liberia_Sierra_Leone_Ebola_Map_August_8_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A situation map of the <a href="/wiki/2014_West_Africa_Ebola_outbreak" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 West Africa Ebola outbreak">Ebola outbreak</a> as of 8 August 2014
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014_Ebola_outbreak">2014 Ebola outbreak</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: 2014 Ebola outbreak">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ebola_virus_epidemic_in_Sierra_Leone" title="Ebola virus epidemic in Sierra Leone">Ebola virus epidemic in Sierra Leone</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Ebola is prevalent in Africa where social and economic inequalities are common. The central African countries are the most prevalent of EVD; like Democratic Republic of Congo, Sudan, Uganda, and Gabon<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2014 there was an outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/2014_West_Africa_Ebola_outbreak" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 West Africa Ebola outbreak">Ebola virus in West Africa</a>. As of 19 October 2014, there had been 3,706 cases of Ebola in Sierra Leone, and 1,259 deaths, including that of the leading physician trying to control the outbreak, <a href="/wiki/Sheik_Umar_Khan" title="Sheik Umar Khan">Sheik Umar Khan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> In early August 2014 <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> closed its borders to Sierra Leone to help contain the spreading of the virus, which originated in Guinea, as more new cases of the disease were being reported in Sierra Leone than in Guinea. Aside from the human cost, the outbreak was severely eroding the economy. By September 2014, with the closure of borders, the cancellation of airline flights, the evacuation of foreign workers and a collapse of cross-border trade, the national deficit of Sierra Leone and other affected countries was widening to the point where the IMF was considering expanding its financial support.<sup id="cite_ref-IMFebola_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMFebola-211">[211]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polygamy">Polygamy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Polygamy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Polygamy_in_Sierra_Leone" title="Polygamy in Sierra Leone">Polygamy in Sierra Leone</a>
      </div> 
      <p>37 per cent of married women in Sierra Leone were in polygamous marriages in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_customs">Food and customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Food and customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leonean_cuisine" title="Sierra Leonean cuisine">Sierra Leonean cuisine</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg/220px-Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg/330px-Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg/440px-Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg 2x" data-file-width="583" data-file-height="380"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sierra_Leone_rice_farming.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Rice farming in Rolako
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Rice is the <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> of Sierra Leone and is consumed at virtually every meal daily. The rice is prepared in numerous ways, and topped with a variety of sauces made from some of Sierra Leone's favourite toppings, including <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> leaves, <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a> leaves, <a href="/wiki/Corchorus" title="Corchorus">crain crain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okra" title="Okra">okra</a> soup, <a href="/wiki/Fried_fish" title="Fried fish">fried fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">groundnut</a> stew.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Along the streets of towns and cities across Sierra Leone one can find foods consisting of fruit, vegetables and snacks such as fresh <a href="/wiki/Mangoes" class="mw-redirect" title="Mangoes">mangoes</a>, oranges, pineapple, fried <a href="/wiki/Plantain_(cooking)" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantain (cooking)">plantains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ginger_beer" title="Ginger beer">ginger beer</a>, fried potato, fried cassava with pepper sauce; small bags of popcorn or peanuts, bread, roasted corn, or skewers of grilled meat or shrimp. </p>
      <p>Poyo is a popular Sierra Leonean drink. It is a sweet, lightly fermented <a href="/wiki/Palm_wine" title="Palm wine">palm wine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> and is found in bars in towns and villages across the country. Poyo bars are areas of lively informal debate about politics, <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>, basketball, entertainment and other issues. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Sierra_Leone" class="mw-redirect" title="Media of Sierra Leone">Media of Sierra Leone</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg/220px-Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg/330px-Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg/440px-Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="317"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Radio_listener_in_Sierra_Leone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A radio listener in <a href="/wiki/Kailahun" title="Kailahun">Kailahun</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Media in Sierra Leone began with the introduction of the first <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> in Africa at the start of the 19th century. A strong free journalistic tradition developed with the creation of a number of newspapers. In the 1860s, the country became a journalist hub for Africa, with professionals travelling to the country from across the continent. At the end of the 19th century, the industry went into decline, and when radio was introduced in the 1930s, it became the primary communication media in the country. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Broadcasting_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierra Leone Broadcasting Service">Sierra Leone Broadcasting Service</a> (SLBS) was created by the colonial government in 1934 making it the earliest English language radio broadcaster service in West Africa. The service began broadcasting television in 1963, with coverage extended to all the districts in the country in 1978. In April 2010, the SLBS merged with the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> peacekeeping radio station in Sierra Leone to form the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Sierra Leone Broadcasting Corporation">Sierra Leone Broadcasting Corporation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> the government-owned current national broadcaster in Sierra Leone. </p>
      <p>The Sierra Leone constitution guarantees <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freedom of the press</a>; however, the government maintains strong control of media, and at times restricts these rights in practice.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> Some subjects are seen as taboo by society and members of the political elite; imprisonment and violence have been used by the political establishment against journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Annual06_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual06-224">[224]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Under legislation enacted in 1980, all newspapers must register with the Ministry of Information and pay sizeable registration fees. The Criminal <a href="/wiki/Libel" class="mw-redirect" title="Libel">Libel</a> Law, including Seditious Libel Law of 1965, is used to control what is published in the media.<sup id="cite_ref-Annual06_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual06-224">[224]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2006, President <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Tejan_Kabbah" title="Ahmad Tejan Kabbah">Ahmad Tejan Kabbah</a> committed to reforming the laws governing the press and media to create a freer system for journalists to work in.<sup id="cite_ref-Annual06_224-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual06-224">[224]</a></sup> As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Sierra Leone is ranked 61st (up two slots from 63rd in 2012) out of 179 countries on Reporters Without Borders' <a href="/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index" title="Press Freedom Index">Press Freedom Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Print_media" class="mw-redirect" title="Print media">Print media</a> is not widely read in Sierra Leone, especially outside <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a> and other major cities, partially due to the low levels of <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a> in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-226">[226]</a></sup> In 2007 there were 15 daily newspapers in the country, as well as those published weekly.<sup id="cite_ref-Jalloh_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jalloh-227">[227]</a></sup> Among newspaper readership, young people are likely to read newspapers weekly and older people daily. The majority of newspapers are privately run and are often critical of the government. The standard of print journalism tends to be low owing to lack of training, and people trust the information published in newspapers less than that found on the radio.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-226">[226]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg/220px-Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg/330px-Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg/440px-Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="349"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Isata_Mahoi_radio_editor_and_actress.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Isata_Mahoi" title="Isata Mahoi">Isata Mahoi</a> shown editing radio programmes in Talking Drum studio <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a>; she is also an actress in the Sierra Leone radio soap opera <i><a href="/wiki/Atunda_Ayenda" title="Atunda Ayenda">Atunda Ayenda</a></i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Radio is the most-popular and most-trusted media in Sierra Leone, with 85% of people having access to a radio and 72% of people in the country listening to the radio daily.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_226-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-226">[226]</a></sup> These levels do vary between areas of the country, with the <a href="/wiki/Western_Area" title="Western Area">Western Area</a> having the highest levels and <a href="/wiki/Kailahun" title="Kailahun">Kailahun</a> the lowest. Stations mainly consist of local commercial stations with a limited broadcast range, combined with a few stations with national coverage – <a href="/wiki/Capital_Radio_Sierra_Leone" title="Capital Radio Sierra Leone">Capital Radio Sierra Leone</a> being the largest of the commercial stations. </p>
      <p>The United Nations Mission in Sierra Leone (UNIOSIL) ran one of the most popular stations in the country, broadcasting programs in a range of languages. The UN mission were restructured in 2008 and it was decided that the UN Radio would be merged with SLBS to form the new Sierra Leone Broadcasting Corporation (SLBC). This merger eventually happened in 2011 after the necessary legislation was enacted. SLBC transmits radio on <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">FM</a> and has two television services, one of which is uplinked by satellite for international consumption. FM relays of <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">BBC World Service</a> (in Freetown, Bo, Kenema and Makeni), <a href="/wiki/Radio_France_Internationale" title="Radio France Internationale">Radio France Internationale</a> (Freetown only) and <a href="/wiki/Voice_of_America" title="Voice of America">Voice of America</a> (Freetown only) are also broadcast. </p>
      <p>Outside the capital Freetown and other major cities, television is not watched by a great many people, although Bo, Kenema and Makeni are served by their own relays of the main SLBC service. There are three free terrestrial television stations in Sierra Leone, one run by the government SLBC and the other two are private stations in Freetown, Star TV which is run by the owner of the <i>Standard Times</i> newspaper and AYV - Africa Young Voices. There are a number of religious funded TV stations that operate intermittently. Two other commercial TV operators (ABC and AIT) closed after they were not profitable. In 2007, a pay-per-view service was also introduced by GTV as part of a pan-African television service in addition to the nine-year-old sub-Saharan Digital satellite television service (DStv) originating from Multichoice Africa in South Africa. GTV subsequently went out of business, leaving DStv as the only provider of subscription satellite television in the country. A number of organisations planned to operate digital terrestrial subscription TV services, with Multichoice's Go TV having built infrastructure ahead of getting a licence and ultimately failing to get a licence. ITV and SATCON are currently operational. </p>
      <p>Internet access in Sierra Leone has been sparse but is on the increase, especially since the introduction of 3G/4G cellular phone services across the country. There are several main <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_providers" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet service providers">internet service providers</a> (ISPs) operating in the country. Freetown has <a href="/wiki/Internet_caf%C3%A9s" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet cafés">internet cafés</a> and other businesses offering internet access. Problems experienced with access to the Internet include an intermittent electricity supply and a slow connection speed in the country outside Freetown. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Art_in_Sierra_Leone" title="Art in Sierra Leone">Art in Sierra Leone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sierra_Leone" title="Music of Sierra Leone">Music of Sierra Leone</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The arts in Sierra Leone are a mixture of tradition and hybrid African and western styles.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> </p> 
      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional"> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px"> 
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:15px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Temne._Ode-Lay_Mask_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Temne._Ode-Lay_Mask_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/76px-Temne._Ode-Lay_Mask_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" decoding="async" width="76" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Temne._Ode-Lay_Mask_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/115px-Temne._Ode-Lay_Mask_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Temne._Ode-Lay_Mask_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/153px-Temne._Ode-Lay_Mask_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="566" data-file-height="886"></a>
          </div>
         </div> 
         <div class="gallerytext"> 
          <p>Odelay mask by <a href="/wiki/Temne_people" title="Temne people">Temne people</a>. <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a>. </p> 
         </div> 
        </div></li> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px"> 
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:15px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Sierra_Leone_Koindu_dance.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Sierra_Leone_Koindu_dance.jpg/79px-Sierra_Leone_Koindu_dance.jpg" decoding="async" width="79" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Sierra_Leone_Koindu_dance.jpg/119px-Sierra_Leone_Koindu_dance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Sierra_Leone_Koindu_dance.jpg/159px-Sierra_Leone_Koindu_dance.jpg 2x" data-file-width="298" data-file-height="450"></a>

          <p>The <a href="/wiki/Koindu" title="Koindu">Koindu</a> dance </p> 
         </div>

      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Sierra_Leone" title="Sport in Sierra Leone">Sport in Sierra Leone</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg/250px-Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg/375px-Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg/500px-Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sierra_Leone_National_Stadium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/National_Stadium_(Sierra_Leone)" title="National Stadium (Sierra Leone)">National Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Association football</a> is by far the most popular sport in Sierra Leone. Children, youth and adult are frequently seen playing <a href="/wiki/Street_football" title="Street football">street football</a> across Sierra Leone. There are organised youth and adult football tournaments across the country, and there are various primary and secondary schools with football teams across Sierra Leone. </p>
      <p>The Sierra Leone national football team, popularly known as the <a href="/wiki/Leone_Stars" class="mw-redirect" title="Leone Stars">Leone Stars</a>, represents the country in international competitions. It has never qualified for the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a> but participated in the <a href="/wiki/1994_African_Cup_of_Nations" title="1994 African Cup of Nations">1994</a> and <a href="/wiki/1996_African_Cup_of_Nations" title="1996 African Cup of Nations">1996 African Cup of Nations</a>. When the national football team, the Leone Stars, have a match, Sierra Leoneans across the country come together united in support of the national team and people rush to their local radio and television stations to follow the live match. The country's national television network, The Sierra Leone Broadcasting Corporation (SLBC) broadcasts the national football team live match, along with many local radio stations across the country. </p>
      <p>When the Leone Stars win an important match, many youth across the county rush to the street to celebrate. Many of the Sierra Leone national team footballers play for teams based in Europe although virtually all of them started professional football in the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_National_Premier_League" title="Sierra Leone National Premier League">Sierra Leone National Premier League</a>. Many of the national team footballers are celebrities across Sierra Leone and they are often well known by the general population. Some of Sierra Leonean international footballers include <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Kallon" title="Mohamed Kallon">Mohamed Kallon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Bangura_(footballer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohamed Bangura (footballer)">Mohamed Bangura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Strasser" title="Rodney Strasser">Rodney Strasser</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kei_Kamara" title="Kei Kamara">Kei Kamara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Teteh_Bangura" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibrahim Teteh Bangura">Ibrahim Teteh Bangura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustapha_Dumbuya" title="Mustapha Dumbuya">Mustapha Dumbuya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Caulker" title="Christian Caulker">Christian Caulker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Bangura" title="Al Bangura">Alhassan Bangura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_Suma" title="Sheriff Suma">Sheriff Suma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Kamara" title="Mohamed Kamara">Mohamed Kamara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umaru_Bangura" title="Umaru Bangura">Umaru Bangura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Julius_Wobay" title="Julius Wobay">Julius Gibrilla Woobay</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_National_Premier_League" title="Sierra Leone National Premier League">Sierra Leone National Premier League</a> is the top professional football league in Sierra Leone and is controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Football_Association" title="Sierra Leone Football Association">Sierra Leone Football Association</a>. Fourteen clubs from across the country compete in the Sierra Leone Premier League. The two biggest and most successful football clubs are <a href="/wiki/East_End_Lions" class="mw-redirect" title="East End Lions">East End Lions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mighty_Blackpool" class="mw-redirect" title="Mighty Blackpool">Mighty Blackpool</a>. East End Lions and Mighty Blackpool have an intense rivalry and when they play each other the national stadium in Freetown is often sold out and supporters of both clubs often clash with each other before and after the game. There is a huge police presence inside and outside the national stadium during a match between the two great rivals to prevent a clash. Many Sierra Leonean youth follow the local football league. </p>
      <p>Many Sierra Leonean youth, children and adults follow the major football leagues in Europe, particularly the English <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a>, Italian <a href="/wiki/Serie_A" title="Serie A">Serie A</a>, Spanish <a href="/wiki/La_Liga" title="La Liga">La Liga</a>, German <a href="/wiki/Bundesliga" title="Bundesliga">Bundesliga</a> and French <a href="/wiki/Ligue_1" title="Ligue 1">Ligue 1</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierra Leone cricket team">Sierra Leone cricket team</a> represents Sierra Leone in international cricket competitions, and is among the best in West Africa. It became an affiliate member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Cricket_Council" title="International Cricket Council">International Cricket Council</a> in 2002. It made its international debut at the 2004 African Affiliates Championship, where it finished last of eight teams. But at the equivalent tournament in 2006, Division Three of the African region of the World Cricket League, it finished as runner-up to <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, and just missed a promotion to Division Two. </p>
      <p>In 2009, the Sierra Leone Under-19 team finished second in the African Under-19 Championship in Zambia, thus qualifying for the Under-19 World Cup qualifying tournament with nine other teams.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> However, the team was unable to obtain Canadian visas to play in the tournament, which was held in Toronto.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Basketball is not a very popular sport in Sierra Leone. The Sierra Leone national basketball team represents Sierra Leone in international men's basketball competitions and is controlled by the Sierra Leone Basketball Federation. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a> (NBA) is popular among a small portion of the youth population. NBA superstars <a href="/wiki/LeBron_James" title="LeBron James">LeBron James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Bryant" title="Kobe Bryant">Kobe Bryant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Durant" title="Kevin Durant">Kevin Durant</a> are popular among Sierra Leone's youthful population. Former NBA stars, in particular <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan" title="Michael Jordan">Michael Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaquille_O%27Neal" title="Shaquille O'Neal">Shaquille O'Neal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allen_Iverson" title="Allen Iverson">Allen Iverson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magic_Johnson" title="Magic Johnson">Magic Johnson</a> are popular in the country. Michael Jordan, in particular, is the most famous basketball player in the country and he is very popular among the general population. Current NBA player <a href="/wiki/Victor_Oladipo" title="Victor Oladipo">Victor Oladipo</a> is of Sierra Leonean descent, as his father is a native of Sierra Leone.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Although tennis is not very popular in the country, up-and-coming American player <a href="/wiki/Frances_Tiafoe" title="Frances Tiafoe">Frances Tiafoe</a> is the son of two Sierra Leoneans who emigrated to the United States. </p>
      <p>Sierra Leone is the first African country to join the <a href="/wiki/International_Floorball_Federation" title="International Floorball Federation">International Floorball Federation</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sierra_Leone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
